       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Cocomilkjf.JPG" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Purest
        <a href="/wiki/Coconut_milk" title="Coconut milk">
         coconut milk
        </a>
        (
        <i>
         kakang gata
        </i>
        ), from 15 fresh, mature coconuts in the
        <a href="/wiki/Philippines" title="Philippines">
         Philippines
        </a>
        .
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Coconut milk, not to be confused with coconut water, is obtained primarily by extracting juice by pressing the grated coconut white kernel or by passing hot water or milk through grated coconut, which extracts the oil and aromatic compounds. It has a fat content of around 23%.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57">
       <a href="#cite_note-57">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        57
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      When refrigerated and left to set,
      <a href="/wiki/Coconut_cream" title="Coconut cream">
       coconut cream
      </a>
      will rise to the top and separate from the milk. The milk can be used to produce virgin coconut oil by controlled heating and removal of the oil fraction.
     </p>
     <p>
      A
      <a href="/wiki/Protein" title="Protein">
       protein
      </a>
      -rich powder can be processed from coconut milk following
      <a href="/wiki/Centrifugation" title="Centrifugation">
       centrifugation
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Separation_process" title="Separation process">
       separation
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Spray_drying" title="Spray drying">
       spray drying
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58">
       <a href="#cite_note-58">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        58
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h4>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Coconut_oil">
       Coconut oil
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Coconut&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Coconut oil">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h4>
     <div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">
      Main article:
      <a href="/wiki/Coconut_oil" title="Coconut oil">
       Coconut oil
      </a>
     </div>
     <p>
      Another byproduct of the coconut is
      <a href="/wiki/Coconut_oil" title="Coconut oil">
       coconut oil
      </a>
      . It is commonly used in cooking, especially for frying. It can be used in liquid form as would other vegetable oils, or in solid form as would butter or lard.
     </p>
     <h4>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Toddy_and_nectar">
       Toddy and nectar
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Coconut&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Toddy and nectar">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h4>
     <p>
      The sap derived from incising the flower clusters of the coconut is drunk as
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Neera" title="Neera">
        neera
       </a>
      </i>
      , also known as toddy or
      <i>
       tuba
      </i>
      (Philippines),
      <i>
       tuak
      </i>
      (Indonesia and Malaysia) or
      <i>
       karewe
      </i>
      (fresh and not fermented, collected twice a day, for breakfast and dinner) in
      <a href="/wiki/Kiribati" title="Kiribati">
       Kiribati
      </a>
      . When left to ferment on its own, it becomes
      <a href="/wiki/Palm_wine" title="Palm wine">
       palm wine
      </a>
      . Palm wine is distilled to produce
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Arrack" title="Arrack">
        arrack
       </a>
      </i>
      . In the Philippines, this alcoholic drink is called
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Arrack#Coconut_arrack" title="Arrack">
        lambanog
       </a>
      </i>
      or "coconut vodka".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lambanog_59-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-lambanog-59">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        59
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The sap can be reduced by boiling to create a sweet syrup or candy such as
      <i>
       te kamamai
      </i>
      in
      <a href="/wiki/Kiribati" title="Kiribati">
       Kiribati
      </a>
      or
      <i>
       dhiyaa hakuru
      </i>
      and
      <i>
       addu bondi
      </i>
      in the Maldives. It can be reduced further to yield
      <a href="/wiki/Coconut_sugar" title="Coconut sugar">
       coconut sugar
      </a>
      also referred to as
      <a href="/wiki/Palm_sugar" title="Palm sugar">
       palm sugar
      </a>
      or
      <a href="/wiki/Jaggery" title="Jaggery">
       jaggery
      </a>
      . A young, well-maintained tree can produce around 300 liters (66impgal; 79U.S.gal) of toddy per year, while a 40-year-old tree may yield around 400 liters (88impgal; 110U.S.gal).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60">
       <a href="#cite_note-60">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        60
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h4>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Heart_of_palm_and_coconut_sprout">
       Heart of palm and coconut sprout
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Coconut&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Heart of palm and coconut sprout">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h4>
     <p>
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Apical_meristem" title="Apical meristem">
       Apical buds
      </a>
      of adult plants are edible, and are known as "palm cabbage" or
      <a href="/wiki/Heart_of_palm" title="Heart of palm">
       heart of palm
      </a>
      . They are considered a rare delicacy, as harvesting the buds kills the palms. Hearts of palm are eaten in salads, sometimes called "millionaire's salad". Newly germinated coconuts contain an edible fluff of marshmallow-like consistency called coconut sprout, produced as the endosperm nourishes the developing embryo.
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Indonesia">
       Indonesia
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Coconut&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Indonesia">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      Coconut is an indispensable ingredient in Indonesian cooking. Coconut meat, coconut milk and coconut water are often used in main courses, desserts and soups throughout the archipelago. In the island of Sumatra, the famous
      <a href="/wiki/Rendang" title="Rendang">
       Rendang
      </a>
      , the traditional beef stew from West
      <a href="/wiki/Sumatra" title="Sumatra">
       Sumatra
      </a>
      , chunks of beef are cooked in coconut milk along with other spices for hours until thickened. In
      <a href="/wiki/Jakarta" title="Jakarta">
       Jakarta
      </a>
      , "Soto Babat" or beef tripe soup also uses coconut milk. In the island of
      <a href="/wiki/Java" title="Java">
       Java
      </a>
      , the sweet and savoury "Tempe Bacem" is made by cooking
      <a href="/wiki/Tempeh" title="Tempeh">
       tempeh
      </a>
      with coconut water,
      <a href="/wiki/Coconut_sugar" title="Coconut sugar">
       coconut sugar
      </a>
      and other spices until thickened. "Klapertart" is the famous Dutch-influenced dessert from Manado, North
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Celebes" title="Celebes">
       Celebes
      </a>
      , that uses young coconut meat and coconut milk. In 2010, Indonesia increased its coconut production. It is now the world's second largest producer of coconuts. The gross production was 15 million tonnes.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FAO_61-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-FAO-61">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        61
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      A sprouting coconut seed is the logo for
      <a href="/wiki/Gerakan_Pramuka_Indonesia" title="Gerakan Pramuka Indonesia">
       Gerakan Pramuka Indonesia
      </a>
      , the Indonesian Scouting organization. It can be seen on all the scouting paraphernalia that elementary (SMA) school children wear as well as on the scouting pins and flags.
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Philippines">
       Philippines
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Coconut&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Philippines">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Coconut_notches.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1776" data-file-width="1000" height="302" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Coconut_notches.jpg/170px-Coconut_notches.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Coconut_notches.jpg/255px-Coconut_notches.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Coconut_notches.jpg/340px-Coconut_notches.jpg 2x" width="170"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Coconut_notches.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Harvesting coconuts in the
        <a href="/wiki/Philippines" title="Philippines">
         Philippines
        </a>
        is done by workers who climb the trees using notches cut into the trunk.
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <div class="thumb tleft">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Coconut2jf.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="959" data-file-width="2639" height="80" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/Coconut2jf.jpg/220px-Coconut2jf.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/Coconut2jf.jpg/330px-Coconut2jf.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/Coconut2jf.jpg/440px-Coconut2jf.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Coconut2jf.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        From left to right: grated, fresh, mature coconut meat; seed interior; healing oil, rare two-eyed coconut shell; and more grated meat (Philippines)
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      The
      <a href="/wiki/Philippines" title="Philippines">
       Philippines
      </a>
      is one of the world's largest producer of coconuts; the production of coconuts plays an important role in the
      <a href="/wiki/Economy_of_the_Philippines" title="Economy of the Philippines">
       economy
      </a>
      . Coconuts in the Philippines are usually used in making main dishes, refreshments and desserts. Coconut juice is also a popular drink in the country. In the Philippines, particularly Cebu, rice is wrapped in coconut leaves for cooking and subsequent storage; these packets are called
      <i>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Puso" title="Puso">
        puso
       </a>
      </i>
      . Coconut milk, known as
      <i>
       gata
      </i>
      , and grated coconut flakes are used in the preparation of dishes such as
      <i>
       laing
      </i>
      ,
      <i>
       ginataan
      </i>
      ,
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Bibingka" title="Bibingka">
        bibingka
       </a>
      </i>
      ,
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Ube_halaya" title="Ube halaya">
        ube halaya
       </a>
      </i>
      ,
      <i>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pitsi-pitsi" title="Pitsi-pitsi">
        pitsi-pitsi
       </a>
      </i>
      ,
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Palitaw" title="Palitaw">
        palitaw
       </a>
      </i>
      ,
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Buko" title="Buko">
        buko
       </a>
      </i>
      and coconut pie.
      <a href="/wiki/Coconut_jam" title="Coconut jam">
       Coconut jam
      </a>
      is made by mixing
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Muscovado_sugar" title="Muscovado sugar">
       muscovado sugar
      </a>
      with coconut milk. Coconut
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bud_sport" title="Bud sport">
       sport fruits
      </a>
      are also harvested. One such variety of coconut is known as
      <i>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Macapuno" title="Macapuno">
        macapuno
       </a>
      </i>
      . Its meat is sweetened, cut into strands and sold in glass jars as coconut strings, sometimes labeled as "gelatinous mutant coconut". Coconut water can be fermented to make a different product
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Nata_de_coco" title="Nata de coco">
        nata de coco
       </a>
      </i>
      (coconut gel).
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Vietnam">
       Vietnam
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Coconut&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Vietnam">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      In
      <a href="/wiki/Vietnam" title="Vietnam">
       Vietnam
      </a>
      , coconut is grown abundantly across
      <a href="/wiki/Central_Vietnam" title="Central Vietnam">
       Central
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Southern_Vietnam" title="Southern Vietnam">
       Southern Vietnam
      </a>
      , and especially in
      <a href="/wiki/B%E1%BA%BFn_Tre_Province" title="Bn Tre Province">
       Bn Tre Province
      </a>
      , often called the "land of the coconut". It is used to make
      <a href="/wiki/Coconut_candy" title="Coconut candy">
       coconut candy
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Caramel" title="Caramel">
       caramel
      </a>
      , and jelly. Coconut juice and coconut milk are used, especially in Vietnam's southern style of cooking, including
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Kho_(cooking_technique)" title="Kho (cooking technique)">
        kho
       </a>
      </i>
      ,
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Ch%C3%A8" title="Ch">
        ch
       </a>
      </i>
      and curry (
      <i>
       c ri
      </i>
      ).
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="India_2">
       India
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Coconut&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: India">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      In southern India, most common way of cooking vegetables is to add grated coconut and then steam them with spices fried in oil. People from southern India also make
      <a href="/wiki/Chutney" title="Chutney">
       chutney
      </a>
      , which involves grinding the coconut with salt, chillies, and whole spices.
      <i>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Uruttu_Chammanthi" title="Uruttu Chammanthi">
        Uruttu chammanthi
       </a>
      </i>
      (granulated chutney) is eaten with rice or
      <i>
       kanji
      </i>
      (rice gruel). It is also invariably the main side dish served with
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Idli" title="Idli">
        idli
       </a>
       ,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vadai" title="Vadai">
        vadai
       </a>
      </i>
      , and
      <i>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dosai" title="Dosai">
        dosai
       </a>
      </i>
      . Coconut ground with spices is also mixed in
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Sambar_(dish)" title="Sambar (dish)">
        sambar
       </a>
      </i>
      and other various lunch dishes for extra taste. Dishes garnished with grated coconut are generally referred to as
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Poduthol" title="Poduthol">
        poduthol
       </a>
      </i>
      in
      <a href="/wiki/North_Malabar" title="North Malabar">
       North Malabar
      </a>
      and
      <i>
       thoran
      </i>
      in rest of Kerala.
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Puttu" title="Puttu">
        Puttu
       </a>
      </i>
      is a culinary delicacy of Kerala and Tamil Nadu, in which layers of coconut alternate with layers of powdered rice, all of which fit into a bamboo stalk. Recently, this has been replaced with a steel or aluminium tube, which is then steamed over a pot. Coconut (
      <a href="/wiki/Tamil_language" title="Tamil language">
       Tamil
      </a>
      :
      <span lang="ta" xml:lang="ta">
       
      </span>
      ) is regularly broken in the middle-class families in Tamil Nadu for food. Coconut meat can be eaten as a snack sweetened with
      <a href="/wiki/Jaggery" title="Jaggery">
       jaggery
      </a>
      or
      <a href="/wiki/Molasses" title="Molasses">
       molasses
      </a>
      . In Karnataka sweets are prepared using coconut and dry coconut "copra"., Like Kaie Obattu, Kobri mitai etc.
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Commercial.2C_industrial.2C_and_household_use">
       Commercial, industrial, and household use
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Coconut&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: Commercial, industrial, and household use">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Coconuts_drying_before_being_processed_into_copra,_Polomuhu_village,_Central_Province,_Solomon_Islands_2004._Photo-_Peter_Davis_-_AusAID_(10687170956).jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2048" data-file-width="3072" height="200" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/Coconuts_drying_before_being_processed_into_copra%2C_Polomuhu_village%2C_Central_Province%2C_Solomon_Islands_2004._Photo-_Peter_Davis_-_AusAID_%2810687170956%29.jpg/300px-Coconuts_drying_before_being_processed_into_copra%2C_Polomuhu_village%2C_Central_Province%2C_Solomon_Islands_2004._Photo-_Peter_Davis_-_AusAID_%2810687170956%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/Coconuts_drying_before_being_processed_into_copra%2C_Polomuhu_village%2C_Central_Province%2C_Solomon_Islands_2004._Photo-_Peter_Davis_-_AusAID_%2810687170956%29.jpg/450px-Coconuts_drying_before_being_processed_into_copra%2C_Polomuhu_village%2C_Central_Province%2C_Solomon_Islands_2004._Photo-_Peter_Davis_-_AusAID_%2810687170956%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/Coconuts_drying_before_being_processed_into_copra%2C_Polomuhu_village%2C_Central_Province%2C_Solomon_Islands_2004._Photo-_Peter_Davis_-_AusAID_%2810687170956%29.jpg/600px-Coconuts_drying_before_being_processed_into_copra%2C_Polomuhu_village%2C_Central_Province%2C_Solomon_Islands_2004._Photo-_Peter_Davis_-_AusAID_%2810687170956%29.jpg 2x" width="300"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Coconuts_drying_before_being_processed_into_copra,_Polomuhu_village,_Central_Province,_Solomon_Islands_2004._Photo-_Peter_Davis_-_AusAID_(10687170956).jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Coconuts drying before being processed into copra in the
        <a href="/wiki/Solomon_Islands" title="Solomon Islands">
         Solomon Islands
        </a>
        .
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Cultivars">
       Cultivars
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Coconut&amp;action=edit&amp;section=38" title="Edit section: Cultivars">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      Coconut has a number of commercial and traditional cultivars. They can be sorted mainly into tall cultivars, dwarf cultivars and hybrid cultivars (hybrids between talls and dwarfs). Some of the dwarf cultivars such as
      <i>
       Malayan dwarf
      </i>
      has shown some promising resistance to
      <a href="/wiki/Lethal_yellowing" title="Lethal yellowing">
       lethal yellowing
      </a>
      while other cultivars such as
      <i>
       Jamaican tall
      </i>
      is highly affected by the same plant disease. Some cultivars are more drought resistant such as
      <i>
       West coast tall
      </i>
      (India) while others such as
      <i>
       Hainan Tall
      </i>
      (China) are more cold tolerant. Other aspects such as seed size, shape and weight and copra thickness are also important factors in the selection of new cultivars. Some cultivars such as
      <i>
       Fiji dwarf
      </i>
      form a large bulb at the lower stem and others are cultivated to produce very sweet coconut water with orange coloured husks (
      <a href="/wiki/King_coconut" title="King coconut">
       king coconut
      </a>
      ) used entirely in fruit stalls for drinking (Sri Lanka, India).
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Coir">
       Coir
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Coconut&amp;action=edit&amp;section=39" title="Edit section: Coir">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">
      Main article:
      <a href="/wiki/Coir" title="Coir">
       coir
      </a>
     </div>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Coconut_wall.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="886" data-file-width="1280" height="152" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/Coconut_wall.jpg/220px-Coconut_wall.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/Coconut_wall.jpg/330px-Coconut_wall.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/Coconut_wall.jpg/440px-Coconut_wall.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Coconut_wall.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        A wall made from coconut husks
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Srilanka_coconut_fibre.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="742" data-file-width="565" height="223" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/Srilanka_coconut_fibre.jpg/170px-Srilanka_coconut_fibre.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/Srilanka_coconut_fibre.jpg/255px-Srilanka_coconut_fibre.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/Srilanka_coconut_fibre.jpg/340px-Srilanka_coconut_fibre.jpg 2x" width="170"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Srilanka_coconut_fibre.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Extracting the fiber from the husk (Sri Lanka)
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Coir (the fiber from the husk of the coconut) is used in ropes, mats, door mats, brushes, sacks, caulking for boats, and as stuffing fiber for
      <a href="/wiki/Mattress" title="Mattress">
       mattresses
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62">
       <a href="#cite_note-62">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        62
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      It is used in
      <a href="/wiki/Horticulture" title="Horticulture">
       horticulture
      </a>
      in potting compost, especially in orchid mix.
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Coconut_fronds">
       Coconut fronds
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Coconut&amp;action=edit&amp;section=40" title="Edit section: Coconut fronds">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="thumb tleft">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Toys_from_coconut.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3000" data-file-width="4000" height="135" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/Toys_from_coconut.jpg/180px-Toys_from_coconut.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/Toys_from_coconut.jpg/270px-Toys_from_coconut.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/Toys_from_coconut.jpg/360px-Toys_from_coconut.jpg 2x" width="180"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Toys_from_coconut.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Toys from coconut leaves
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      The stiff mid-ribs of coconut leaves are used for making brooms in India, Indonesia (
      <i>
       sapu lidi
      </i>
      ), Malaysia, the Maldives and the Philippines (
      <i>
       walis tingting
      </i>
      ). The green of the leaves (lamina) are stripped away, leaving the veins (wood-like, thin, long strips) which are tied together to form a broom or brush. A long handle made from some other wood may be inserted into the base of the bundle and used as a two-handed broom. The leaves also provide material for baskets that can draw well water and for roofing thatch; they can be woven into mats, cooking skewers, and kindling arrows, as well. Two leaves (especially the younger, yellowish shoots) woven into a tight shell the size of the palm are filled with rice and cooked to make
      <i>
       ketupat
      </i>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63">
       <a href="#cite_note-63">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        63
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Dried coconut leaves can be burned to ash, which can be harvested for
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lime_(mineral)" title="Lime (mineral)">
       lime
      </a>
      . In India, the woven coconut leaves are used as
      <i>
       pandals
      </i>
      (temporary sheds) for marriage functions especially in the states of Kerala, Karnataka, and Tamil Nadu.
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Copra">
       Copra
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Coconut&amp;action=edit&amp;section=41" title="Edit section: Copra">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">
      Main articles:
      <a href="/wiki/Copra" title="Copra">
       Copra
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Coconut_oil" title="Coconut oil">
       Coconut oil
      </a>
     </div>
     <p>
      Copra is the dried meat of the seed and after processing produces coconut oil and coconut meal. Coconut oil, aside from being used in cooking as an ingredient and for frying, is used in soaps, cosmetics, hair-oil, and massage oil. Coconut oil is also a main ingredient in
      <a href="/wiki/Ayurveda" title="Ayurveda">
       Ayurvedic oils
      </a>
      . In Vanuatu coconut palms for
      <a href="/wiki/Copra" title="Copra">
       copra
      </a>
      production are generally spaced 9 meters apart, allowing a tree density of 100160 trees per hectare.
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Husks_and_shells">
       Husks and shells
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Coconut&amp;action=edit&amp;section=42" title="Edit section: Husks and shells">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      The husk and shells can be used for fuel and are a source of
      <a href="/wiki/Charcoal" title="Charcoal">
       charcoal
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64">
       <a href="#cite_note-64">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        64
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Activated carbon manufactured from coconut shell is considered extremely effective for the removal of impurities. The coconut's obscure origin in foreign lands led to the notion of using cups made from the shell to neutralise poisoned drinks. The cups were frequently engraved and decorated with precious metals.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65">
       <a href="#cite_note-65">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        65
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      A dried half coconut shell with husk can be used to buff floors. It is known as a
      <i>
       bunot
      </i>
      in the Philippines and simply a "coconut brush" in
      <a href="/wiki/Jamaica" title="Jamaica">
       Jamaica
      </a>
      . The fresh husk of a brown coconut may serve as a dish sponge or body sponge.
     </p>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Coconut_button_factory_in_Dongjiao_-_02.JPG">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3000" data-file-width="4000" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/80/Coconut_button_factory_in_Dongjiao_-_02.JPG/220px-Coconut_button_factory_in_Dongjiao_-_02.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/80/Coconut_button_factory_in_Dongjiao_-_02.JPG/330px-Coconut_button_factory_in_Dongjiao_-_02.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/80/Coconut_button_factory_in_Dongjiao_-_02.JPG/440px-Coconut_button_factory_in_Dongjiao_-_02.JPG 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Coconut_button_factory_in_Dongjiao_-_02.JPG" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Coconut buttons in Dongjiao Town,
        <a href="/wiki/Hainan" title="Hainan">
         Hainan
        </a>
        , China
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      In Asia, coconut shells are also used as bowls and in the manufacture of various handicrafts, including buttons carved from dried shell. Coconut buttons are often used for Hawaiian
      <a href="/wiki/Aloha_shirt" title="Aloha shirt">
       aloha shirts
      </a>
      .
      <i>
       Tempurung
      </i>
      as the shell is called in the Malay language can be used as a soup bowl andif fixed with a handlea ladle. In Thailand, the coconut husk is used as a potting medium to produce healthy forest tree saplings. The process of husk extraction from the coir bypasses the retting process, using a custom-built coconut husk extractor designed by
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ASEAN" title="ASEAN">
       ASEAN
      </a>
      Canada Forest Tree Seed Centre (ACFTSC) in 1986. Fresh husks contains more
      <a href="/wiki/Tannin" title="Tannin">
       tannin
      </a>
      than old husks. Tannin produces negative effects on sapling growth.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66">
       <a href="#cite_note-66">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        66
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In parts of South India, the shell and husk are burned for smoke to repel mosquitoes.
     </p>
     <p>
      Half coconut shells are used in theatre
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Foley_sound_effects" title="Foley sound effects">
       Foley sound effects
      </a>
      work, banged together to create the sound effect of a horse's hoofbeats. Dried half shells are used as the bodies of musical instruments, including the Chinese
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Yehu" title="Yehu">
        yehu
       </a>
      </i>
      and
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Banhu" title="Banhu">
        banhu
       </a>
      </i>
      , along with the Vietnamese
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/%C4%90%C3%A0n_g%C3%A1o" title="n go">
        n go
       </a>
      </i>
      and Arabo-Turkic
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Rebab" title="Rebab">
        rebab
       </a>
      </i>
      . In the Philippines, dried half shells are also used as a music instrument in a folk dance called
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Maglalatik" title="Maglalatik">
        maglalatik
       </a>
      </i>
      .
     </p>
     <p>
      In World War II,
      <a href="/wiki/Coastwatchers" title="Coastwatchers">
       coastwatcher
      </a>
      scout
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Biuki_Gasa" title="Biuki Gasa">
       Biuki Gasa
      </a>
      was the first of two from the
      <a href="/wiki/Solomon_Islands" title="Solomon Islands">
       Solomon Islands
      </a>
      to reach the shipwrecked and wounded crew of
      <a href="/wiki/Motor_Torpedo_Boat_PT-109" title="Motor Torpedo Boat PT-109">
       Motor Torpedo Boat PT-109
      </a>
      commanded by future U.S. president
      <a href="/wiki/John_F._Kennedy" title="John F. Kennedy">
       John F. Kennedy
      </a>
      . Gasa suggested, for lack of paper, delivering by dugout canoe a message inscribed on a husked coconut shell. This coconut was later kept on the president's desk, and is now in the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/John_F._Kennedy_Library" title="John F. Kennedy Library">
       John F. Kennedy Library
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       [
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
         <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2011)">
          citation needed
         </span>
        </a>
       </i>
       ]
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Coconut_trunk">
       Coconut trunk
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Coconut&amp;action=edit&amp;section=43" title="Edit section: Coconut trunk">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:132px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Cocos_nucifera_at_Kudayathoor.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3648" data-file-width="2736" height="173" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Cocos_nucifera_at_Kudayathoor.jpg/130px-Cocos_nucifera_at_Kudayathoor.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Cocos_nucifera_at_Kudayathoor.jpg/195px-Cocos_nucifera_at_Kudayathoor.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Cocos_nucifera_at_Kudayathoor.jpg/260px-Cocos_nucifera_at_Kudayathoor.jpg 2x" width="130"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Cocos_nucifera_at_Kudayathoor.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        The base of an old coconut palm
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Coconut_Palace_Court.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1138" data-file-width="1500" height="167" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Coconut_Palace_Court.jpg/220px-Coconut_Palace_Court.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Coconut_Palace_Court.jpg/330px-Coconut_Palace_Court.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Coconut_Palace_Court.jpg/440px-Coconut_Palace_Court.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Coconut_Palace_Court.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        <a href="/wiki/Coconut_Palace" title="Coconut Palace">
         Coconut Palace
        </a>
        ,
        <a href="/wiki/Manila" title="Manila">
         Manila
        </a>
        , Philippines, built entirely out of coconut and local materials
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Coconut trunks are used for building small bridges and huts; they are preferred for their straightness, strength, and salt resistance. In Kerala, coconut trunks are used for house construction.
      <a href="/wiki/Coconut_timber" title="Coconut timber">
       Coconut timber
      </a>
      comes from the trunk, and is increasingly being used as an ecologically sound substitute for endangered hardwoods. It has applications in furniture and specialized construction, as notably demonstrated in Manila's
      <a href="/wiki/Coconut_Palace" title="Coconut Palace">
       Coconut Palace
      </a>
      .
     </p>
     <p>
      Hawaiians hollowed the trunk to form drums, containers, or small canoes. The "branches" (leaf
      <a href="/wiki/Petiole_(botany)" title="Petiole (botany)">
       petioles
      </a>
      ) are strong and flexible enough to make a
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Switch_(rod)" title="Switch (rod)">
       switch
      </a>
      . The use of coconut branches in corporal punishment was revived in the Gilbertese community on Choiseul in the
      <a href="/wiki/Solomon_Islands" title="Solomon Islands">
       Solomon Islands
      </a>
      in 2005.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67">
       <a href="#cite_note-67">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        67
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Coconut_roots">
       Coconut roots
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Coconut&amp;action=edit&amp;section=44" title="Edit section: Coconut roots">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      The roots are used as a dye, a
      <a href="/wiki/Mouthwash" title="Mouthwash">
       mouthwash
      </a>
      , and a medicine for diarrhea and
      <a href="/wiki/Dysentery" title="Dysentery">
       dysentery
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Grimwood18_8-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Grimwood18-8">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        8
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      A frayed piece of root can also be used as a toothbrush.
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Use_in_beauty_products">
       Use in beauty products
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Coconut&amp;action=edit&amp;section=45" title="Edit section: Use in beauty products">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      Coconuts are used in the beauty industry in moisturisers and body butters because coconut oil, due to its chemical structure, is readily absorbed by the skin. The coconut shell may also be ground down and added to products for
      <a href="/wiki/Exfoliation_(cosmetology)" title="Exfoliation (cosmetology)">
       exfoliation
      </a>
      of dead skin. Coconut is also a source of
      <a href="/wiki/Lauric_acid" title="Lauric acid">
       lauric acid
      </a>
      , which can be processed in a particular way to produce
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sodium_lauryl_sulfate" title="Sodium lauryl sulfate">
       sodium lauryl sulfate
      </a>
      , a detergent used in shower gels and shampoos.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68">
       <a href="#cite_note-68">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        68
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The nature of lauric acid as a fatty acid makes it particularly effective for creating detergents and
      <a href="/wiki/Surfactant" title="Surfactant">
       surfactants
      </a>
      .
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Role_in_culture_and_religion">
       Role in culture and religion
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Coconut&amp;action=edit&amp;section=46" title="Edit section: Role in culture and religion">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <p>
      In the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ilocos_region" title="Ilocos region">
       Ilocos region
      </a>
      of northern Philippines, the
      <a href="/wiki/Ilocano_people" title="Ilocano people">
       Ilocano people
      </a>
      fill two halved coconut shells with
      <i>
       diket
      </i>
      (cooked sweet rice), and place
      <i>
       liningta nga itlog
      </i>
      (halved boiled egg) on top of it. This ritual, known as
      <i>
       niniyogan
      </i>
      , is an offering made to the deceased and one's ancestors. This accompanies the
      <i>
       palagip
      </i>
      (prayer to the dead).
     </p>
     <p>
      A coconut (
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sanskrit_language" title="Sanskrit language">
       Sanskrit
      </a>
      :
      <span lang="sa" xml:lang="sa">
       <i>
        nalikera
       </i>
      </span>
      ) is an essential element of
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rituals" title="Rituals">
       rituals
      </a>
      in
      <a href="/wiki/Hindu" title="Hindu">
       Hindu
      </a>
      tradition. Often it is decorated with bright metal foils and other symbols of auspiciousness. It is offered during worship to a Hindu god or goddess. Irrespective of their religious affiliations, fishermen of India often offer it to the rivers and seas in the hopes of having bountiful catches. Hindus often initiate the beginning of any new activity by breaking a coconut to ensure the blessings of the gods and successful completion of the activity. The Hindu goddess of well-being and wealth,
      <a href="/wiki/Lakshmi" title="Lakshmi">
       Lakshmi
      </a>
      , is often shown holding a coconut.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69">
       <a href="#cite_note-69">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        69
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In the foothills of the temple town of
      <a href="/wiki/Palani" title="Palani">
       Palani
      </a>
      , before going to worship
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Murugan" title="Murugan">
       Murugan
      </a>
      for the
      <a href="/wiki/Ganesha" title="Ganesha">
       Ganesha
      </a>
      , coconuts are broken at a place marked for the purpose. Every day, thousands of coconuts are broken, and some devotees break as many as 108 coconuts at a time as per the prayer. In
      <a href="/wiki/Tantra" title="Tantra">
       tantric
      </a>
      practices, coconuts are sometimes used as substitutes for human skulls.
      <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       [
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
         <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2014)">
          citation needed
         </span>
        </a>
       </i>
       ]
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In
      <a href="/wiki/Hindu_wedding" title="Hindu wedding">
       Hindu wedding
      </a>
      ceremonies, a coconut is placed over the opening of a pot, representing a
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Womb" title="Womb">
       womb
      </a>
      . Coconut flowers are auspicious symbols and are fixtures at
      <a href="/wiki/Hindu" title="Hindu">
       Hindu
      </a>
      and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Buddhist" title="Buddhist">
       Buddhist
      </a>
      weddings and other important occasions. In Kerala, coconut flowers must be present during a marriage ceremony. The flowers are inserted into a barrel of unhusked rice (paddy) and placed within sight of the wedding ceremony. Similarly in
      <a href="/wiki/Sri_Lanka" title="Sri Lanka">
       Sri Lanka
      </a>
      , coconut flowers, standing in brass urns, are placed in prominent positions.
     </p>
     <p>
      The
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zulu_Social_Aid_and_Pleasure_Club" title="Zulu Social Aid and Pleasure Club">
       Zulu Social Aid and Pleasure Club
      </a>
      of
      <a href="/wiki/New_Orleans" title="New Orleans">
       New Orleans
      </a>
      traditionally throws hand-decorated coconuts, the most valuable of Mardi Gras souvenirs, to parade revelers. The "Tramps" began the tradition
      <i>
       circa
      </i>
      1901. In 1987, a "coconut law" was signed by
      <a href="/wiki/Edwin_Edwards" title="Edwin Edwards">
       Gov. Edwards
      </a>
      exempting from insurance liability any decorated coconut "handed" from a Zulu float.
     </p>
     <p>
      The coconut is also used as a target and prize in the traditional British fairground game "coconut shy". The player buys some small balls which he throws as hard as he can at coconuts balanced on sticks. The aim is to knock a coconut off the stand and win it.
     </p>
     <p>
      It was the main food of adherents of the now discontinued Vietnamese religion
      <a href="/wiki/Coconut_Religion" title="Coconut Religion">
       o Da
      </a>
      in
      <a href="/wiki/B%E1%BA%BFn_Tre" title="Bn Tre">
       Bn Tre
      </a>
      .
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Myths_and_legends">
       Myths and legends
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Coconut&amp;action=edit&amp;section=47" title="Edit section: Myths and legends">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      Some South Asian, Southeast Asian and Pacific Ocean cultures have
      <a href="/wiki/Origin_myth" title="Origin myth">
       origin myths
      </a>
      in which the coconut plays the main role. In the
      <a href="/wiki/Hainuwele" title="Hainuwele">
       Hainuwele
      </a>
      myth from
      <a href="/wiki/Maluku_Islands" title="Maluku Islands">
       Maluku
      </a>
      , a girl emerges from the blossom of a coconut tree.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70">
       <a href="#cite_note-70">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        70
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In
      <a href="/wiki/Maldivian_folklore" title="Maldivian folklore">
       Maldivian folklore
      </a>
      one of the main myths of origin reflects the dependence of the
      <a href="/wiki/Maldivians" title="Maldivians">
       Maldivians
      </a>
      on the coconut tree.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NIAS_Press_71-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-NIAS_Press-71">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        71
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      According to
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Death_by_coconuts" title="Death by coconuts">
       an urban legend
      </a>
      , there are more deaths caused by falling coconuts than by sharks annually.
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Other_uses">
       Other uses
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Coconut&amp;action=edit&amp;section=48" title="Edit section: Other uses">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Srilanka_coconut_rug.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="394" data-file-width="532" height="163" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/Srilanka_coconut_rug.jpg/220px-Srilanka_coconut_rug.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/Srilanka_coconut_rug.jpg/330px-Srilanka_coconut_rug.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/Srilanka_coconut_rug.jpg/440px-Srilanka_coconut_rug.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Srilanka_coconut_rug.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Making a rug from coconut fiber
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      The leftover fiber from coconut oil and coconut milk production, coconut meal, is used as livestock feed. The dried
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Calyx_(botany)" title="Calyx (botany)">
       calyx
      </a>
      is used as fuel in wood-fired stoves. Coconut water is traditionally used as a growth supplement in plant tissue culture/micropropagation.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72">
       <a href="#cite_note-72">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        72
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The smell of coconuts comes from the 6-pentyloxan-2-one molecule, known as delta-decalactone in the food and fragrance industries.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73">
       <a href="#cite_note-73">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        73
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Tool_and_shelter_for_animals">
       Tool and shelter for animals
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Coconut&amp;action=edit&amp;section=49" title="Edit section: Tool and shelter for animals">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      Researchers from the
      <a href="/wiki/Melbourne_Museum" title="Melbourne Museum">
       Melbourne Museum
      </a>
      in Australia observed the
      <a href="/wiki/Octopus" title="Octopus">
       octopus
      </a>
      species
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Amphioctopus_marginatus" title="Amphioctopus marginatus">
        Amphioctopus marginatus
       </a>
      </i>
      <a href="/wiki/Tool_use_by_animals" title="Tool use by animals">
       use of tools
      </a>
      , specifically coconut shells, for defense and shelter. The discovery of this behavior was observed in
      <a href="/wiki/Bali" title="Bali">
       Bali
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/North_Sulawesi" title="North Sulawesi">
       North Sulawesi
      </a>
      in Indonesia between 1998 and 2008.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74">
       <a href="#cite_note-74">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        74
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AP_75-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-AP-75">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        75
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-scientificamerican_76-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-scientificamerican-76">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        76
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <i>
       Amphioctopus marginatus
      </i>
      is the first
      <a href="/wiki/Invertebrate" title="Invertebrate">
       invertebrate
      </a>
      known to be able to use tools.
      <i>
       <b>
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AP_75-1">
         <a href="#cite_note-AP-75">
          <span>
           [
          </span>
          75
          <span>
           ]
          </span>
         </a>
        </sup>
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Times_77-0">
         <a href="#cite_note-Times-77">
          <span>
           [
          </span>
          77
          <span>
           ]
          </span>
         </a>
        </sup>
       </b>
      </i>
     </p>
     <p>
      A coconut can be hollowed out and used as a home for a rodent or small birds. Halved, drained coconuts can also be hung up as bird feeders, and after the flesh has gone, can be filled with fat in winter to attract
      <a href="/wiki/Tit_(bird)" title="Tit (bird)">
       tits
      </a>
      .
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Allergies">
       Allergies
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Coconut&amp;action=edit&amp;section=50" title="Edit section: Allergies">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Food_allergies">
       Food allergies
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Coconut&amp;action=edit&amp;section=51" title="Edit section: Food allergies">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      Coconut can be a food allergen although its prevalence varies from country to country. While coconut is one of the top-five food allergies in India where it is a common food source,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78">
       <a href="#cite_note-78">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        78
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      such allergies to coconut are considered rare in Australia, the UK, and the United States.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-allergy.org.au_79-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-allergy.org.au-79">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        79
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      As a result, commercial extracts of coconut are not currently available for
      <a href="/wiki/Skin_allergy_test#Prick_test" title="Skin allergy test">
       skin prick testing
      </a>
      in Australia or New Zealand.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_80-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-80">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        80
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Despite a low prevalence of allergies to coconut in the United States, the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/U.S._Food_and_Drug_Administration" title="U.S. Food and Drug Administration">
       U.S. Food and Drug Administration
      </a>
      (FDA) began identifying coconuts in October 2006.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-allergy.org.au_79-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-allergy.org.au-79">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        79
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Based on FDA guidance and federal U.S. law, coconut must be disclosed as an ingredient.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81">
       <a href="#cite_note-81">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        81
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Topical_allergies">
       Topical allergies
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Coconut&amp;action=edit&amp;section=52" title="Edit section: Topical allergies">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      Coconut-derived products can cause
      <a href="/wiki/Contact_dermatitis" title="Contact dermatitis">
       contact dermatitis
      </a>
      . They can be present in cosmetics, including some shampoos, moisturizers, soaps, cleansers and hand washing liquids. Those known to cause contact dermatitis include:
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Coconut_diethanolamide" title="Coconut diethanolamide">
       coconut diethanolamide
      </a>
      , cocamide sulphate, cocamide DEA, CDEA,
      <a href="/wiki/Sodium_laureth_sulfate" title="Sodium laureth sulfate">
       sodium laureth sulfate
      </a>
      , sodium lauroyl sulfate, ammonium laureth sulfate, ammonium lauryl sulfate, sodium lauroyl sarcosinate, sodium cocoyl sarcosinate, potassium coco hydrolysed collagen, triethanolamine laureth sulfate, caprylic/capric triglycerides, triethanolamine lauryl or cocoyl sarcosime, disodium oleamide sulfocuccinate, laureth sulfasuccinate, and disodium dioctyl sulfosuccinate.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_80-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-80">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        80
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
